# Google Cloud Professional Machine Learning Engineer Cert Prep

> Earning the Google Cloud Professional Machine Learning Engineer certification confirms that you’re able to build, evaluate, productionize, and optimize AI solutions by using Google Cloud capabilities and knowledge of conventional ML approaches. In this course, Noah Gift prepares you for the certification, starting with an overview of the exam—including the format of the exam, the time it should ta

#### [Framing ML problems: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-1-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-1-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Tutor] Framing Machine Learning Problems. Let's talk about the key terminology in this particular lesson. First up, in terms of key terms here we have machine learning. Machine learning is a powerful tool that helps businesses automate tasks, make better decisions and also improve efficiency and serve out predictions. Supervised machine learning is a common approach for tasks like classification and regression where data has known labels or values. Unsupervised learning is useful when we don't have labeled data or if you want to make a way of discovering hidden patterns in data, like clustering is a good example. You could discover customer clusters. [[Reinforcement Learning]] is used in cases where an agent must learn how to make decisions in complex, dynamite environments. A good example would be robotics, gaming or even resource allocation. Let's talk about other key terms like ML Solution Readiness. ML solution readiness means that it's an assessment of whether a machine learning solution is feasible and you would consider factors like data availability, quality, limitations, [[Responsible AI]] practices, and even potential risks. There's also ML use cases and what this means is that there's specific problems or tasks that machine learning can solve or even automate with a business context. This includes customer segmentation, fraud detection
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-1-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=96)** or even demand [[Forecasting]]. In terms of machine learning problem types these are different categories of machine learning problems, including classification, progression, clustering, and these determine the structure of the input data and the desired output. In terms of business success criteria these include specific measurable goals or outcomes that indicate whether an ML solution is successful in addressing the business problem. So for example, improving accuracy, reducing a cost or even increasing efficiency. Another key term here is Bias in ML. Bias in ML allows you to look at things like fair, unbiased or accurate model predictions, and would these align with, for example, [[Google]]'s responsible AI practices? There's also business impact assessment, which is crucial for determining the feasibility of an ML solution and also communicating its potential effects to stakeholders. Data readiness, this means ensuring that data is useful for a machine learning solution and that the quality is there, the quantities there and also that there's a format for successful model training and deployment. If it's not in the right format you're not going to be able to train the model. Also, in terms of responsible AI, is it going to be designed in a way that's ethical and trustworthy so that you can avoid potential negative consequences and even legal consequences.
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-1-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=189)** All right. That's it for the discussion about key terms in problem framing. These are some things that come up quite a bit. It's important to understand the ML use cases and problem types when you're selecting the right algorithm to achieve the desired outcome, and also if you want to design business success criteria and assess the business impact to ensure the ML solutions are aligned with business goals and drive value, you have to understand these key terms. And also it's important to understand how to evaluate an ML solution and provide data readiness so that you can look at the risks in the project lifecycle. Finally, it's important to adhere to responsible AI practices and address the biases in ML models so that you can build ethical, trustworthy and fair AI systems.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

### 1. Translating Business Challenges into ML Use Cases

#### [Building AI-enabled workflows](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-ai-enabled-workflows?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-ai-enabled-workflows?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Here we see an example of how AI tools, including things like [[Hugging Face]], OpenAI, all come together to build AI enabled automation. Here's an example. Let's say a publisher takes a YouTube video and transcribes it automatically to English. They could then use Hugging Face to summarize it and create a title. They could also use Hugging Face to summarize a paragraph. They could use a either a zero-shot classification tool from Hugging Face or a tool like Yake to get keyword paragraphs. And they could build all this automation up by using this off-the-shelf AI tool. They could put those into a [[Google Drive]] directory or they could upload them automatically and they could build this as their first version of the pipeline. Later, as they could tack onto the functionality of these AI tools, they could transcribe to the 10 most popular languages their videos are generated from. They could also build their own recommendation engine using NLP, for example, to find other videos to recommend to their users. They could also then programmatically build all this stuff out into a title, a summary, a link to YouTube video. And then you could programmatically update this to YouTube. So there are a lot of automation factors when you use a pre-trained model, like OpenAI or Hugging Face, that are really kind of hidden behind the scenes, but when you put it all together like this, you realize that AI actually enables automation and they're very complimentary tools.

#### [Using AI tools to build AI tools](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/using-ai-tools-to-build-ai-tools?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/using-ai-tools-to-build-ai-tools?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at this modern AI programmer workflow. In this particular workflow, AI creates AI to talk to AI, and how do we do this? Well, to start out with, if you look at [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]], a cloud-based development environment, you can go in and configure it with customized Dockerfile, customized .devcontainer file. So it's really optimized for doing AI programming and machine learning programming so that you can use GPUs. One of the things that you can do when you're building this is actually talk to [[GitHub Copilot]]. Now what is it? Well, it's an AI peer programming utility that communicates with OpenAI Codex. And this is a pre-trained model that is designed to help you autocomplete your code and it's intelligent enough to give you code suggestions. Now, what's happening when you're using this is that you could also communicate with Cloud SDKs. For example, you could build out a new system that transcribes code or that builds out things that talk to, let's say, the S3 ecosystem and AWS. And what this means is that you are meta-programming using AI. In fact, you are using AI that was pre-trained to help create [[AI Solutions]]. And this is really the modern AI programmer workflow is that AI is in fact an enabler that enables you to build code faster, use higher level workflows, and also move at a faster pace
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/using-ai-tools-to-build-ai-tools?u=76281980&t=99)** while you're building out things in the machine learning space. So it's important to consider how you can use AI tools like Github Copilot or AWS CodeWhisper when you're building out solutions.

### 2. Defining ML Problems

#### [Simulations vs. experiment tracking](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/simulations-vs-experiment-tracking?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/simulations-vs-experiment-tracking?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Here we have a difference between simulations and [[MLOps]] Experiment Tracking. There are actually a lot of similarities. If we take a look at a [[Simulation]], like the Traveling Salesman Problem, the idea is you run multiple times through, let's say, a greedy, random start algorithm and you solve for the minimum distance. So what is the shortest amount of distance I would travel to to go to all the cities I need to travel? Likewise, with a roulette wheel, if you wanted to maximize revenue, you could create a bunch of simulations and see if there's a way to, you know, get a great outcome from a roulette wheel. Well, as we know, there is no optimal outcome, but if you wanted to see it yourself, you can actually go through here and figure out a simulation. Likewise, poker, you could build your own poker simulation to maximize revenue. Or if you were looking for a startup that you wanted to join, you'd see how the venture capital portfolios maximize their revenue by minimizing their risk. That's why they invest in a hundred companies and then they push all the risk to the startup employees. Now, if you take a look at Machine Learning Operations Experiment tracking, it's a very similar process. You look through experiments you want to minimize error across a bunch of different experiments, or optimize for some metric, you potentially want to do a bunch of hyperparameter tuning and see which one is the best tuning job. So the classic simulation and the MLOps Experiment Tracking have a lot in common. Let's go ahead and take a look at both of those.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/simulations-vs-experiment-tracking?u=76281980&t=98)** Here, I have a repository that has multiple simulations that I've created. The first one I want to take a look at is a roulette simulator. This is kind of a fun one. If I wanted to simulate 10,000 runs of a roulette wheel, where I bet on the color black each time, what would happen? This is really a way to simulate the law of large numbers, so you can see that, in fact, you don't have a great chance of winning. If we go through here and we run this, you can see that I'm going to bet a dollar and I'm going to select the color black. I'm going to run tons and tons of simulations. And you can see these simulations. I can even visualize everything that's happening. And what we see is that, in fact, I get negative winnings and it shows the number of blacks, the number of reds, and the number of greens. If I run, want to run the simulation again, I'll also get a negative outcome because of the law of large numbers. Eventually, you can see that, over time, I lose money playing roulette. Now, if we want to take a look at that real quick, let's take a look at the roulette simulation here, which is right here. The basic gist of the code is that I use [[Pandas (Software)|pandas]], I use click, and I build a wheel out, first. Then, what I do is I have a spin_wheel. I have a simulate_wheel. I have a report that gets generated. I calculate my winnings across the simulation. Here, we have red, black, green, et cetera. I print things out and then finally, I build it out into a command line tool,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/simulations-vs-experiment-tracking?u=76281980&t=192)** which we can see here. So that's really the genesis of mini simulations. Now, if I want to run a new simulation, that's a different kind. We can also do a poker simulation. Let's go ahead and do that. If I go through here and I run a poker simulation, here we go, how much do you want to bet? Let's just bet a dollar, and we'll bet it five times. And each time it shows me the hand. It shows me whether I won. And then at the very end, it says the expected value was $4 based on my bets, but I was able to win a $100. And this is a great way to actually simulate different ways that you play a certain hand. And again, if we want to take a look at the code real quick here, it's going to follow that same pattern as I build out whatever it is, the world that I'm going to simulate, in this case, the deck of cards. I would go through here and build some helper code that would do things, like deal the code. I would pick certain hands so that I would know which ones I was betting against. I would build things out, and then I would have my display. And then finally, I would have the evaluation here. And then at the very, very end, I would do the simulation. Now, I have one more that I'll show, which is a fun one, which is, let's say we wanted to simulate what it's like to be a venture capitalist. And if I go through here and I run that, we also can see here that, oh, look, I was able to get a 20% return on my investment, or I was able to get an 80% return on my investment. And you can see that the cumulative investment was, you know, $500 and then the payout was was $2,885 million.
>
> **[4:49](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/simulations-vs-experiment-tracking?u=76281980&t=289)** And it shows you each of the percentage owns of each of these 100 companies that they invested in. And so you can see here that in this particular simulation it did well. Now, what if I simulated lots of different startup employees, right? Because they're getting the risk pushed to them and they don't have the ability to leverage lots of different companies to work for. You can see that, in fact, in this scenario here, that the [[Probability]] of a startup being successful is 2%, right, over and over again. Right, so this is really an idea that you'll see a lot, is that by doing simulations, you're able to actually go ahead and figure out what it is you're trying to build and minimize or maximize for something. So in practice, what this means, is that if I go over to Databricks here and I wanted to take a look at an experiment tracking run that I'm doing here, you can see here that I would go through here and I could actually see the differences, right? I would go through and look at the metrics that I'm trying to fill out, and that would show up in this experiment tracking tab here. So really in a nutshell, the concept of experiment tracking is very, very similar to many things that you would do when you're actually doing machine learning operations. And by doing simulations, you're able to actually really turn the knob on moving from a simulation-style workflow to an MLOps-style workflow.

#### [When to use ML](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/when-to-use-ml?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/when-to-use-ml?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] When do you use machine learning versus another technique? One of the easy ways to take a look at this scenario is if the problem can be solved by heuristic, you should use a heuristic first. Here's a good example. We have maybe a location-based problem where a shipping company needs to visit the shortest distance to travel to a series of cities. This is called the traveling salesman problem, a classic problem in computer science. This would be much better served as a heuristic, and there are many known solutions here, and it's easy to debug what's happening. You could say, I want to do a random greedy start, run multiple simulations, and then minimize for the shortest distance between the route that you would travel. Likewise, if you have labeled historical data, in this case, let's say you have a notebook. You pull together, and you have the labeled points per game, and you have the label salary, and you wanted to predict the salary in the future of what you would pay a NBA player based on how many points per game they scored. This would be a great use case for machine learning. In general, if you can solve a problem with a heuristic, it's best to start with that first and see if that is a good solution versus jumping directly into machine learning and trying to do some complicated approach. Likewise, if you have tabular data, for example, and it's labeled, and you want to predict one thing
>
> **[1:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/when-to-use-ml?u=76281980&t=105)** based on the results of another column, this would be a great use case for doing machine learning in a business scenario.

#### [Supervised vs. unsupervised ML](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/supervised-vs-unsupervised-ml?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/supervised-vs-unsupervised-ml?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] There are some key differences between unsupervised machine learning and supervised machine learning. Let's first take a look at supervised machine learning. It works by taking labeled historical data and using that historical data to make a prediction. In the case of a regression problem, here, we have points per game. If we wanted to use that to predict a salary, we would be able to create a supervised machine learning prediction based on that label of points, which would predict the target of salary. So in any scenario where you have labeled data, especially tabular data like this that's in a numerical form, you can solve it by doing supervised machine learning. In the case of unsupervised machine learning, the key idea here is that you're going to discover hidden patterns, and those could then become the labels. Here's a great scenario that we could take a look at by using clustering, and we would cluster on points, assists, and rebounds, you would be able to discover these hidden patterns. For example, who are the highest scorers in the National Basketball Association? Who are the point guards, who are the centers? And you could figure this out because when you cluster, they'll naturally group together in multi-dimensional space. So, between these three axes, these values will be the nearest to each other, and then the domain expert
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/supervised-vs-unsupervised-ml?u=76281980&t=103)** will be able to discover these hidden patterns. Typically, with unsupervised machine learning, it's best to have a domain expert there to really put a notation on the hidden pattern so that you can then apply that label to the data set. And that's really the key difference, discovery versus working on a problem where you already have the labeled data and you're doing a prediction. Now, the two can work together as well because in the case of unsupervised machine learning, this could be the first step is find the labels for the data, and then the next step would be to use that data to do a prediction.

#### [Optimization](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/optimization?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/optimization?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Speaker] One of the foundational problems in [[Deep Learning]] is this concept of optimizing. And let's take a look at a few different scenarios here that explain this principle. When you're trying to solve for a problem there are local minima and global minima and if you get stuck in a local minima you're not going to get the ultimate best solution. So in this scenario here, if you're slowly descending down and you get stuck inside of this local minima then you won't eventually get to this global minima down here and get the best possible solution which reduces the error rate of the classification problem you're trying to solve for example. One of the intuitions behind this is to think about walking down a mountain in the dark. You could get stuck in a lake somewhere halfway down the mountain. That's the local minima. But if you get to the very bottom then that would be the global minimum. Another way to think about this problem is to think about the problem of gradient descent which is that you're building a loss function that you want to get better and better accuracy. You want to reduce the loss and eventually you need to get into the learning rate. So how much do you learn as you're moving down to find the global minimum? If you set a learning rate that's too small, then it'll take forever to get to the solution.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/optimization?u=76281980&t=100)** To get to the global minimum. If you take a learning rate that's too big right here you would bounce over past and shoot past the the global minimum. So one of the things to consider when you're solving deep learning problems is this learning rate and you can actually find out what your learning rate is by trial and error. And a really good tool for that is to use the [[TensorFlow]] playground and it gives you a great starting point to play around with learning rates and also experimenting with loss functions.

#### [Clustering](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/clustering?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/clustering?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] Let's talk through the core mathematical principles of clustering. The way we can look at this in a very straightforward way is to look at the season [[Statistics]] for the NBA. We have points, rebounds, and assists. In a typical game where someone does an amazing job and they get 10 points, 10 rebounds, and 10 assists, this is called a triple double because it's so rare. But we can look at these three values and find natural groupings if we plot them in a multi-dimensional space. So if we see here, on one axis we have points, another axis, we have assists, another, we have rebounds, and we assign essentially a cluster to each natural grouping where the points, rebounds, and assists are close to each other, you'll see that natural groupings appear. So in this case, we have some scorers here that score a lot of points, but also have a reasonable amount of assists. Because they're so far away from the basket, they're not going to get a lot of rebounds, so you can see that they're naturally over here. Meanwhile, the point guards are going to lead in assists, and so you see they're all grouped together here, and they get a decent amount of rebounds, but they're not as much of a rebounder as the centers, who are down towards the basket and getting the rebounds and really focused on that particular job. So you can see here that when you cluster things in multi-dimensional space, there'll be an emergent label that appears, and it could be the scorers,
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/clustering?u=76281980&t=102)** the point guards, the centers. It really depends on the dataset, but this is really the core intuition. And in fact, you can try it yourself by going to an NBA dataset and doing [[k-means clustering]], and you'll find that there'll be really easy natural groupings that will apply to what you commonly would think. Someone would be, let's say, a point guard, a forward, or some other position on the court.

### 3. Defining Business Success Criteria

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [Defining business success criteria](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/defining-business-success-criteria?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/defining-business-success-criteria?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] When you're designing a machine learning project, it's important to consider the business success criteria. Let's take a look at some of these key characteristics of a successful machine learning project. First up, we have data availability and quality. A model is going to need data to make accurate predictions and decisions. The business needs to have access to large volumes of high quality, relevant, and diverse data to train and optimize a model. And also, it's going to take some time and resources to clean, pre-process, and label the data. So this is an important consideration when designing a successful project. Second, we have a clear business objective or a business goal. You want to define and measure an achievable goal for your machine learning project. Make sure that the business strategy is aligned with this, and that the company's growth and competitiveness will be enhanced by using this particular machine learning project, so that a customer satisfaction is improved or operational cost is improved, or increasing market share. Another one that's important is that the skilled workforce that you have, data scientists, machine learning engineers, domain experts, that they're actually ready to go and that you can use these people in your organization and the best tools in order to get a good result. Also, efficient use of resources is critical. Are you going to be able to use GPUs, CPUs, or TPUs,
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/defining-business-success-criteria?u=76281980&t=102)** or you know, any kind of a custom silicone or cloud-based service in a way that doesn't cost more than the, you know, really the results that you're getting from the machine learning project? And that really gets us down to the ROI, which is you have to analyze the financial feasibility of the machine learning project. Make sure that you're taking into account the cost of [[Data Acquisition]], also infrastructure, personnel, maintenance. And you have to compare those ROI alternatives as well, like if you use a pre-trained model or use an API would that actually be a better for, you know, the brand reputation or customer loyalty or marketing positioning? So it's a trade off analysis when you're going through and making a machine learning project. You don't always have to build something yourself. It's important to understand where you should compete and where you should actually buy a solution from a third party vendor.

### 4. Identifying Risks to Feasibility of ML Solutions

[↑ Back to Table of Contents](#table-of-contents)

#### [MLOps hierarchy of needs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/mlops-hierachy-of-needs?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/mlops-hierachy-of-needs?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's dive into the concept of [[MLOps]] Hierarchy of Needs. Really, it is like a pyramid, and that at the bottom is [[DevOps]]. If you don't have DevOps, there's no possibility of an organization doing MLOps. And some of the key components of DevOps include [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|infrastructure as code]], right? Are you able to automatically provision environments and check that into your build system? Can you do [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] of your software stack? So are [[Microservices]] being delivered in the staging environment and then automatically being propagated to production? And also, have you designed around a build system? Next step, we have Data Operations. So similarly, once you've gone through and you've got that base level of DevOps, you have to look at your [[Data Management]] platforms. And really, are you using a platform? Are you using something like [[Google]], BigQuery, or Databricks, or [[Snowflake]], or Amazon Athena; some platform that makes a serverless query and visualization workflow very straightforward and also has the ability to do data jobs and data tasks. The next layer up then would be to use some kind of MLOps platform. You don't want to be building things yourself and going through and taking a lot of time away from your core business goals. So there are things like feature stores that can store the curated features that you can use. You can use specific model serving platforms. You can do experiment tracking with tools that are designed
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/mlops-hierachy-of-needs?u=76281980&t=98)** to help you look at different metrics and different explanatory techniques, and then also take a look at things like data drift where you can look at the measuring impact of a particular model and production and see has the actual underlying data changed. Finally, once you've got these three things, you're at that MLOps layer. Now, in order to fully implement the MLOps layer though, you need to think about two things from a business perspective. One, the business ROI, so is the machine learning model that you're creating, is it adding value to the organization, that's the business ROI. And then second, are you framing the problem correctly? If you don't frame the problem correctly, it's possible that you're solving the wrong problem for your organization. Once you've done that, you can do things like [[Forecasting]], looking at predicting the correct amount of inventory, using things like unsupervised machine learning to discover new patterns. And really, this is just an automation workflow. That's really what MLOps is is just going through step-by-step and automating the stack from the software stack to the data stack, to the platform stack, to ultimately the MLOps stack which serves as an accelerant to the business value that's already there in an organization. This is the MLOps Hierarchy of Needs.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[MLOps]] (8), [[DevOps]] (4), [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as code]] (1), [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|Continuous delivery]] (1), [[Microservices]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** roi (2)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Hidden costs of bespoke systems](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/hidden-costs-of-bespoke-systems?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/hidden-costs-of-bespoke-systems?u=76281980&t=5)** - Here we have the cost of a free or bespoke system. This happens a lot in big companies. You have a brilliant engineer who really is talented and they build a solution for the organization that solves a particular problem. That bespoke solution is not, though, in the core business of that particular enterprise. Let's say you're a telecom company and you're building a search engine that's used internally or you're building a machine learning operations platform and your company doesn't sell ML ops platforms. One of the problems is that, initially, it works, so in year one, it could do amazing things, but then what happens is that brilliant engineer, according to the laws of microeconomics, will be soon leaving for a huge payday at a trillion dollar company. And what that happens to do is the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of your company has a problem, because let's say there's an outage later, let's say after three months from when the employee leaves, it'll then be replaced by a proprietary system because the organization doesn't have the skills that that brilliant engineer had in order to maintain the system, and not everybody knows how to build that system and it isn't in the core of the business. Later, the proprietary system comes in, the organization that then has to retrain on the proprietary system in year two, and the new system delivers actually slightly better results than the one that was built internally. So this is one of the problems of building things, even if it is actually done correctly,
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/hidden-costs-of-bespoke-systems?u=76281980&t=100)** is that it's dependent on a particular individual or a particular team when a better solution could have been implemented from the beginning where it's actually going to prevent the system being under the guise of repair constantly and needing new people to fix it, and instead, it can actually be maintained by third party software vendors and you can train people on that system. So there's a lot to think about when you're building something or buying something. Many organizations should really lean towards buying something because of the fact that you can get the maintenance and support and you're not focusing on things that are outside of your core business.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|Rest]] (1)

#### [Data poisoning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-poisoning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-poisoning?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at some insider threats that could occur via data poisoning. This is often not looked at in terms of a threat as what could an insider do into your organization and this is why it's important to carefully consider the concept of principle of least privilege. So let's say in this first attack vector here, we have a corporate-owned system, let's say [[Google Drive]], email, et cetera. And we know that there's a particular type of image that could cause your Google Drive to go offline. So what could happen is that someone inside a corporation could willfully put a prohibited image inside of the Google Drive which would then make sure that the corporation is offline. And it would take some time for that organization to be able to contact [[Google]] and explain exactly what happened. So this could be an extended "denial of service" attack on a corporation, as the corporation tries to turn the Google assets back online. So this is actually pretty sneaky, but easy to do, depending on what privileges a employee has in an organization. They know that, in fact, they could even put something that is very hard to find out that it's a prohibited image. Maybe it's a World War II image or something like that. Now, a second one that is an attack vector is that potentially an insider could secretly seed training data with images
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-poisoning?u=76281980&t=103)** that are known to trigger some kind of forbidden attack. And so in this case, they could plant a file on a customer system and that system would train the data and eventually the multi-class classification model would see something that wasn't really there. They could plant essentially a false flag of forbidden content. And that particular organization could constantly get a denial of service attack because an insider was explicitly trying to take out another organization. So in both cases here, the training data that's planted, or in the case of the prohibited image, one of the ways that this could be mitigated is through regular auditing, reducing the principle of least privilege approach. So making sure that people don't have more access than they need to. These are not theoretical attacks. It's very important to consider data poisoning as a real threat to many organizations as data and email gets more popular.

#### [Architecting a ML solution: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-2-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-2-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** Architecting ML solutions. Key terminology. Let's talk through the key terms for this lesson. First up, we have ML services. These are [[Cloud Services]] designed for ML tasks. These could include cloud build or even Kubeflow. [[Data Collection]]. This means gathering raw data for processing and analysis. [[Data Management]] includes organizing, storing, and maintaining collected data. Exploration and analysis. This includes examining and understanding data patterns and trends. We also have key terms, including automation and orchestration. These include utilizing tools and processes designed to reduce manual intervention and repetitive tasks. And then in terms of orchestration, this includes coordinating and managing the execution of multiple tasks in a workflow. [[Feature Engineering]] includes creating and selecting relevant input features from raw data. And logging and management includes capturing and storing system logs and other relevant data for analysis. We also have compute and accelerator options. With compute, this would include different types of processing units like CPU or GPU or even TPU. In terms of accelerator options, it's specifically talking about specialized [[Hardware]] and this would include TPU and
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-2-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=94)** edge based devices. Monitoring includes observing and tracking the performance of ML systems and applications, and then serving includes deploying and providing access to trained ML models for inference. There also includes Personally Identifiable Information. Let's talk about that. That's data that can be used to identify an individual like a Social Security number or driver's license. And then in terms of protected health information or PHI, this is personal health information protected by [[Privacy]] laws and regulations. For secure machine learning systems, the goal here is to implement security measures to protect data and models from unauthorized access and unintentional exploitation. And privacy implications include understanding and addressing the potential privacy risks associated with data usage and collection. All right. That's it for this particular lesson. In terms of key terms, it's important to understand architecting ML solutions and understand the key terms that we discussed, including [[Data Privacy]] in particular. That's one to watch out for.

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/cloud-developer-workspace-advantage?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's take a look at this Cloud Developer Workspace advantage. It's one of the more interesting new trends that we're seeing in many fields: software engineering, [[Data Engineering]], [[Data Science]], machine learning engineering, also machine learning operations. In some sense, if you're not using a cloud based developer environment, you're really missing out on many new features that are not available on your laptop. First, let's take a look at a laptop. In this case, we have a laptop or a workstation and you can see that it's non deterministic, right? There is no guaranteed environment, there could be packages that are installed that you don't want, it could also be very costly to get GPUs and to have, let's say, a solid state drive that is large, and this is not going to be the same as where your current deployment environment is running, right? If you're running in the cloud, it's not going to be the same as your laptop, with some exceptions, you know, you can work around that with containers. Now, if we go to the cloud here and we take a look at some of these cloud based environments and really we can see that [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]], AWS Cloud9, GCP Cloud IDE, [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]] Cloud IDE, all of these have many things in common and that there is typically an IDE that's customized for development. It also has easy integration with [[GitHub Copilot]],
>
> **[1:41](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/cloud-developer-workspace-advantage?u=76281980&t=101)** which allows you to write code where the OpenAI Codex is essentially telling you what to write. And also this whole integration is very tightly coupled with GitHub. So this is one of the favorite environments that I have. I think it's a great environment to develop in. And if we go down to the bottom here, we also see that there are these notebook based environments. We have CoLab Notebooks, the CoLab Notebooks allow you to build in the cloud, Jupyter Compatible systems that use GPUs, you can even use a pro version. There's also [[AWS SageMaker]] Studio Lab, which allows people to go through and run Jupyter Notebooks using GPUs that are free that are provided by the AWS platform. So these are really good options for notebook friendly workflows. If we take a look at Cloud9 here, it's got customized features, including role based privileges. You don't have to put your API key inside, and it also has tight integration with other services, like, for example, AWS Cloud, Lambda AWS, S3, API Gateway, those kinds of services that allow you to build serverless applications. But there's a lightweight version which is called AWS CloudShell, and this also is a good environment for even doing very
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/cloud-developer-workspace-advantage?u=76281980&t=197)** minor software engineering. And also you can switch between Bash, Azure Shell, or [[Powershell]]. If you go into other clouds, they're very similar, right? And that GCP has its own cloud editor and also has a Cloud Shell, Azure has its own cloud editor and also has a Cloud Shell. So one thing that you'll start to notice is that the world is moving towards these powerful, disposable preloaded environments that have deep integration with cloud. They have SDKs installed and they're co-located in the same location. So if you're in a coffee shop somewhere and you want to work with terabyte of data, well, you're out of luck if you have to copy that data back and forth. But if the data is already in the cloud and you spin up a cloud based development environment, you're co-located directly with that data. So really the future is, a Cloud Developer Workspace Advantage and it's something that I would recommend people dive into.

#### [What is continuous delivery?](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's walk through a [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] workflow using the latest cloud native ecosystem tools. To start off with, you would develop in an environment. Oftentimes this would be a cloud based environment like Cloud9 or AWS or [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]] on the [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]] platform. And the reason for using these cloud native environments is that they give you the ability to use all of the developer tools that will later be in the production environment. So you have a very similar one to one environment. Now, when you're building things, you'll push not only the software but the [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as Code]] as well. And the Infrastructure as Code allows you to define every single aspect of what's happening in a cloud based environment or a [[Kubernetes]] based environment. And notice here that when you push the changes that the build system will then go through and test your code, merge the code, and this build will then automatically go to the provisioning step. And what's powerful about Infrastructure as Code is that it's idempotent. And what this means is that it only makes a change if a change is necessary, but it also makes the state always be in the same condition. So if you initially create the infrastructure, when you do another, maybe a second build, it won't create the infrastructure again because it'll notice that, let's say, this S3 bucket here is already been created, so it won't need to make that change. Now, likewise, if you mutate it, if you go to your Infrastructure as Code,
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/what-is-continuous-delivery?u=76281980&t=98)** you make a small change, it would then go through here and create a new bucket for you. Finally, if you need to clean up your resources, it's easiest to do this with Infrastructure as Code. Now, finally, another thing you can do that's pretty powerful here, is you can do whatever actions you'd like. So you can go ahead and say instead of a delete action, you could say, I want to create a new infrastructure here and I want to build out in this particular environment a stage environment. And this staging environment is where I'll do my [[Load Testing]]. And then you could likewise make another fork of the code and the Infrastructure as Code and then create a production environment. So this idea of using the infrastructure itself to define the system and to make idempotent changes is a huge part of continuous delivery, and this allows you to constantly push the changes into a new environment. It doesn't necessarily mean that you will always push that into a production environment without a human being involved, but it does actually allow you to make pushes to multiple environments, and this is really the key concept in continuous delivery.

#### [Containerized ML microservices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/containerized-ml-microservices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/containerized-ml-microservices?u=76281980&t=5)** Here is a containerized machine learning [[Microservices]] idea. And first we have [[Linux]] and Bash. In this particular example, we could be interacting with Linux and Bash with a command line tool. We could be using containers with Linux and Bash. We also could be using a cloud based environment. These could include AWS Cloud9 or for example, [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]]. And this is where I would build out my container. Now when I was ready to deploy, I could hook up into the microservice framework for the cloud provider. These are provided via container registries where I would register my container and then I would do a [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] process that would deploy to one of the cloud platforms continuous delivery [[PaaS|Platform as a Service]] offerings. And here's a good example, AWS App Runner is a microservice friendly framework that allows you to take a container and push it out automatically, either through a build system like AWS Codebuild or through your own build system like GitHub Actions. The [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] has a similar container based deployment called GCP Cloud Run, and [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]] App Services also has a similar service that allows you to do containerized workflows. There's also another step that you could add, and that's by integrating with an [[MLOps]] platform. So what this means is that, potentially you could deploy some models by interacting with an API call and then calling an inference
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/containerized-ml-microservices?u=76281980&t=100)** endpoint that's sitting out somewhere and this deployment target is then using that MLOps platform to make your API calls. Another thing you could do is, your microservice may need to interact with the experiment tracking service. Potentially you could have an internal tool that's working with experiment tracking and potentially looking at the latest version of a model that has the best metrics and automatically picking that model for the interaction with the Platform as a Service offering. Now, the big thing to take away with this kind of a workflow is that the container makes things possible. The container by packaging the runtime as long side the code allows you to do these really efficient microservice based continuous delivery. So if you are going to build microservices, I think these three platforms, AWS App Runner, GCP Cloud Run, and Azure App Services are a great place to start.

#### [Reproducible workflow](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/reproducible-workflow?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/reproducible-workflow?u=76281980&t=8)** A reproducible workflow involves [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as Code]] in addition to components like a build system and a source code repository. Here, we have an Infrastructure as Code workflow where we take things from our either cloud based development environment or local development environment like Cloud9 or [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]] and we do the test merge trigger build workflow. So this is testing our code, making sure that Linting is working and making sure the deployment system works. And then in terms of the Infrastructure as Code, behind the scenes it could be creating infrastructure like S3 bucket, for example. It could also not make a change. So if nothing has changed, when you do the next deploy, it'll just keep the state of the S3 bucket the same. If you do need to make a change, for example, change an S3 bucket to a static website, you could actually deploy a mutated infrastructure change. And then if you needed to delete your change, you could delete and destroy your infrastructure, like, for example, getting rid of an S3 bucket. So really the idea behind Infrastructure as Code is that there isn't a human pushing buttons that make something happen. The code itself is infrastructure and it's put into source control and it's part of the build process, and so you always know what is exactly happening in your infrastructure because you can look in your repo and look at the build system and see what the changes were that were reflected on the last push that you made.

#### [Selecting heavy vs. light MLOps](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-heavy-vs-light-mlops?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-heavy-vs-light-mlops?u=76281980&t=6)** Let's take a look at a couple of different types of [[MLOps]] workflows. In this first example here we have more of a batch or a static workflow where you would take something directly from [[GitHub]], potentially even the model lives in GitHub because it's a very small model. You would build it with a cloud native build system, in this case [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] Build, and then there's a pass or a [[PaaS|Platform as a Service]] offering, in this case [[Google]] App engine, and you would serve out a prediction and then this would be your AI API. So this is a very lightweight kind of hobbyist workflow, although also it could work very well with maybe pre-trained model workflows. In a more sophisticated MLOps workflow, you could call this a heavy workflow, and there's really a data centric type approach versus a model centric type approach. In the data centric approach, the data is, maybe, the most important aspect of what you're building. You maybe don't even do any modelling at all and you could go through and use AutoML pointed at a data set, and then create a model, and then later deploy it. Now, notice there are some components of an MLOps system, a platform. In this case, this is [[Vertex]] AI. There's other AI platforms like, for example, SageMaker. There's also -- Databricks has a MLOps/AI platform. But in general, they all do similar things in that there's data labeling,
>
> **[1:37](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-heavy-vs-light-mlops?u=76281980&t=97)** there's data drift detection. And again, data drift is something where you could tell whether the data has changed underneath the hood and then trigger a new training run and then a new model. There's feature stores where you've got the features that are collected in a way that are really convenient to make new models. You have the -- all the models actually registered. You have model monitoring to make sure that it's performing correctly. You have experiment tracking so you can see the accuracy of the different systems that have been created and actually go back in time and recreate things. You have explainable AI and these systems like the shapely extension, can show which features are driving which model, and then finally, you have the prediction component. So all of these are features that really depend on what it is you're doing. Are you more of a data centric or a more a model centric? And then finally, you have pipeline orchestration, like the entire process of building the system and you have notebooks and containers. These are all components that often go together in an MLOps system. And then underneath the hood, this is also not necessarily talked about enough, but in any modern system, most likely you're going to have a [[Data Management]] system on the Google Cloud. BigQuery is one of the systems that people often use to query the data. In fact, you can even do machine learning directly inside of it. So this is really like a high level overview of some of the main things to consider when building an MLOps system. Do you want to go light,
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-heavy-vs-light-mlops?u=76281980&t=192)** do you want to go heavy, do you want to pick data centric, or do you want to pick model centric?

#### [Key components of MLOps landscape](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/key-components-of-mlops-landscape?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/key-components-of-mlops-landscape?u=76281980&t=5)** Let's take a look at this cloud [[MLOps]] landscape here. It's important to know all of the key factors when doing MLOps. Really, if you are doing machine learning operations, you're going to most likely use something in the cloud, even if you don't use their cloud MLOps platform. Let's take a look at some of the things that are available. One, you have elastic storage systems, so you can scale up and down, do object storage, maybe mounted storage using a file system. This is now becoming an emerging thing, in fact, is that many cloud providers are actually giving you mount points that the whole cluster can actually read and write to that don't have to actually use behind the scenes some kind of a [[Hadoop]] or Spark based system. I think this is a trend we're going to see more and more of. And as a result you get near infinite access to disk IO, storage, CPU, GPUs, even specialized chips, ASCIS that allow you to do things like maybe run [[TensorFlow]] inference on a TPU. The elastic compute systems here, again, have the ability to expand according to your needs. You also have built on top of these, are these serverless and containerized managed services. So this is the network effect and that cloud providers can provide things like AWS App Runner or AWS Lambda, or [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] functions, and these build on top of the existing compute and storage. There's also these integrated tools and SDK. You'll see that they're available in Cloud Shells or cloud based

And there's really a shift of developer centric and this would be things to the left here on AWS, for example, or [[Microsoft Azure|Azure]] or GCP, they have these Cloud Shells. They also have cloud IDEs, more like a Visual Studio type environment. And then as you get a little bit more into the data and the machine learning side, you'll see that every vendor has a storage query tool and a dashboard. A good example would be Amazon has Athena where you can do [[SQL]] queries against object storage. [[Google]] has BigQuery, where you can do SQL queries against their distributed storage system. And then finally, as you get into the machine learning phase here, you'll see that there's Jupyter Notebook based platforms, there's MLOps platforms. Almost all cloud providers have this concept of an MLOps platform experiment tracking, model registry, feature store, inference. Now, it may not be exactly what you'd like and so that's why there are also specialized solutions. So you can think of these as the kind of the core building blocks. And a lot of times the third party vendors will then tap into those core capabilities. So there's DataOps, you know, you can look at things like Databricks or [[Snowflake]]'s, MLops as well. There's lots of MLOps platforms, specialized [[Databases]], maybe graph databases or time series databases, monitoring and observability, you know, things like Datadog

These are all third party vendors that fit really tightly into the cloud environment, but also allow you to expand beyond it to make a specialized solution. So really, when someone is thinking of MLOps, they need to also be aware of the entire ecosystem behind MLOps and choose what are the things that are more commodity that you want to actually use and what are the specialized services where you want to pay a vendor, you know, a premium because they give you a premium ROI.

#### [Feature store vs. data warehouse](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/feature-store-vs-data-warehouse?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/feature-store-vs-data-warehouse?u=76281980&t=5)** A data warehouse and a feature store have different characteristics. On one hand, we can look at this picture from a low quality data angle. So if you take a look at a data lake here, it could be a combination of streaming data, batch data, [[Unstructured Data]], structured data. So you really have to do a lot of work to clean this up. Now, in this particular problem, what could you do? Well, you could take that data that's raw inside of the data lake and do featurization and put together high quality cacheable reusable features inside of a feature store that would then be used for training, prediction, clients, also going through and auditing, potentially developing automatic correlations. All of these things are a component of a feature store. So really it's a refinement of what's in the data lake so that the machine learning systems have this high quality access to features that will be used to train models. Another different kind of resource is a data warehouse. And so the idea here is it's more shifted for reporting [[Dashboards]], [[Business Intelligence (BI)|business intelligence]], but in both categories, either feature store or a data warehouse, you can see that it's high quality data. The one difference here is that the business intelligence side is on the data warehouse component and the AI/ML side is on the feature store. So there isn't necessarily a hard or fast rule, there could be some overlap between a feature store and a data warehouse.
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/feature-store-vs-data-warehouse?u=76281980&t=99)** But really the idea here is you're migrating the lower quality data that's unstructured and not cleaned and converting automation tools like ETL pipelines and featurization pipelines into these high quality resources so you can create more useful value for your organization.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Business Intelligence (BI)|Business intelligence]] (2), [[Unstructured Data]] (1), [[Dashboards]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** etl (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Analogies:** picture (1)

#### [Compute choice](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/compute-choice?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/compute-choice?u=76281980&t=8)** Here, let's talk about how to select the GPU versus CPU for the training job that you're running or for the inference job. First, let's take a look at a CPU here, it is a cheap and single purpose built chip. And so what this means is that it's great for doing things like opening up spreadsheets, but it's less great for doing parallel processing because it's not designed to be parallel. On the other hand, the GPU has high density, so it's designed to be parallel, but it has a higher cost and the higher cost allows you to do things in parallel. So there's a trade off here between a CPU and a GPU and it really depends on what kind of problem you're trying to solve, how fast you want to solve it. And really, this is one of the things to consider is if you can use a CPU, maybe time isn't something that you care about, it can be done, let's say, in batch and it's not urgent, a CPU could be a good choice. If you need it to be very performant, you're doing a huge parallel operation, then a GPU could be a good choice because it could speed up the amount of time it takes to train something or the inference time.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Env Vars:** cpu (5), gpu (4)
> **UI Navigation:** select the (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)

#### [Architecting a ML solution: Next steps](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/next-steps-24563458?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/next-steps-24563458?u=76281980&t=0)** GCP Professional Machine Learning Engineer. Let's talk through this conclusion. Course 2: Architecting ML Solutions. First up, in Course 2, we talked about a [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] in machine learning. This means ensuring rapid, reliable, and consistent deployment of ML models. We also talked about containerizing ML [[Microservices]] such as creating modular, scalable, and maintainable ML services and using tools like Docker and [[Kubernetes]]. We also talked about reproducible workflows, including consistency and repeatability of ML workflows. [[Continuous Integration (CI)|Continuous integration]], automating the integration of code changes from multiple contributors. We also talked through heavy versus light [[MLOps]]. This means understanding the trade offs between resource intensive and lightweight MLOps solutions. We also dove into what is the difference between a feature store versus a data warehouse and compared the purposes and benefits of a feature store and a data warehouse. And we also talked through compute choices. This means selecting the right [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] [[Hardware]] components for specific needs, and also balancing performance cost and efficiency. All right. That's it for this course. #### [Designing data preparation and processing systems: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-three-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-three-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** Designing [[Data Preparation]] and processing systems. Let's dive into the key terminology in this section. First up, we have the key terms, including Exploratory Data Analysis or EDA. EDA is the process of visually and statistically examining a dataset to understand its structure, uncover patterns, and also identify potential problems or insights. This is a first step when you're doing machine learning. Really get a view of what's happening with the data. Now, [[Data Quality]] is a system that refers to accuracy or consistency. Another way to look at it is completeness of a data set, and this ensures that it's suitable for its intended purpose. Another key term to identify is [[TensorFlow]] [[Data Validation]]. In this case this would be TFDV, and this is a library that helps you understand, validate, and monitor the input data at scale. It can provide descriptive [[Statistics]]. Also, it can dive into data anomalies and also data drift detection. [[Data Pipelines]] are a series of [[Data Processing]] steps that move, transform, and validate data from its raw form to a format suitable for analysis or machine learning. Also in terms of key terms, we have [[Feature Engineering]], and this is a process of creating or modifying input features to improve the performance of ML models.
>
> **[1:36](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-three-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=96)** Class imbalance is when a distribution of classes in a data set is not equal, and the problem with this is it could lead to biased predictions by the machine learning model. We also have feature crosses. Feature crosses are a combination of two or more features that can provide additional information to a machine learning model. One of the reasons you do this is it can improve predictive performance. We also have TensorFlow Transform or TFT. A TFT is a library for preprocessing input data with TensorFlow. This can enable the application of the same transformation logic, bolstering the training and the serving of the ML model. So it really provides a level of uniformity across the entire pipeline of [[MLOps]]. All right. So these are some of the key terms that we covered in this section, which include designing data preparation and processing systems.

An alternate way is if you go to the command prompt and you type in the cmd command to get it started and then type in Python, what you'll notice is that it will automatically install Python from the [[Microsoft]] Store for you. And notice again, the Python Software Foundation is the official distributor of Python, not a third party company. Now, if I go through here and I type in python -m venv, it includes a virtual environment manager right inside of the Python library. And if I type in venv, it'll create one inside of this directory. Now, let's say you wanted to do [[Data Science]]. So this is a common thing that people on Windows want to do. No fear. You don't have to use any other tools other than Python itself. If we cd into this directory and we look inside, notice there's a scripts directory and inside of here all I have to do is type in activate.bat, and if I type in activate.bat, what will happen? It gives me a virtual environment.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-science-setup-with-virtualenv-and-pip-on-windows?u=76281980&t=100)** Now that I've got this virtual environment distributed from the Python Software Foundation, all I need to do is do my installations and they'll stay in this environment. Now, if I wanted to install [[Pandas (Software)|pandas]], let's go ahead and do that. Here we go. We've got pandas installed here. Again, a seamless workflow without needing to install any third party tools. I just did a pip install pandas. Oftentimes, if you're doing data science, people are going to want to use things other than just pandas. And so a lot of times you'll use Jupyter. Let's go ahead and install that as well. And here we say pip install Jupyter. Now again, I think this is a surprise to most people is that the Python standard library has all of the tools necessary without needing to go to any other tool, even though those tools might be useful for other people, all you need is the Python language itself. And what it will do is, once you've activated the virtual environment, you can install any package you want just by using the regular Python tools. There we go. So now that we've got this activated, let's go ahead and run Jupyter Notebook. Here we go. We have Jupyter Notebook. And what's amazing about this is I've got this thing running right here. And look, here's my Jupyter Notebook. Let's go ahead and do some data science from here. #### [Labeling data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/labeling-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/labeling-data?u=76281980&t=8)** Here, we can see an example of working with labeled data. If I go through and grab some data out of a notebook, the Points-Per-Game column would be the label. That label would be also a feature, right? So there's two different terms for this particular column, and I could use this feature to predict a target. The target could be the salary of an NBA player. So one of the things I may want to determine is based on the Points-Per-Game, what would be a salary that I could arrange for that NBA player? Let's say I was the owner of an NBA team. And the intuition here is fairly straightforward in that whatever it is that you're trying to use to predict a target is a feature and it will need to have a particular label. If you don't have the label, you may have to get the label using another approach. You can either use human workers like, for example, using Mechanical Turk, or you could use unsupervised machine learning to create natural groupings, which then you could use to label the data. In general, though, all machine learning problems will require a feature that has a label to it, if you're going to do a supervised machine learning problem.

#### [Mechanical Turk labeling](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/mechanical-turk-labeling?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/mechanical-turk-labeling?u=76281980&t=8)** Here we have a Mechanical Turk labeling system based on a real system that I developed in the past. First, we have the sports box scores here, and you can see that in this particular scenario, we could grab out the athletes name. And then what we could do is we could ask a quorum of workers, let's say, 10 workers across the globe to find social media handles for that athlete. Once they agree on what the social media handles are, let's say, they all find out, let's say, seven out of ten of them agree that a particular Twitter handle or a [[LinkedIn]] handle is the handle that is that athletes handle, then we could pull that into a [[Data Collection]] system and then feed that into a machine learning system that would predict the influence of that particular athlete. So you can see here there's a multi-step process that first starts with getting some known data set, then feeding it into a group of human workers who are then going to come together as a quorum, and then later it's going to lead to a data collection system that uses that cleaned up data and labeled data. And then finally, after the data has been pulled, you can run a machine learning model. So there are multiple steps in the real world to use a system like Mechanical Turk, but it's invaluable depending on what kind of problem you're trying to solve.

> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/cleaning-up-data?u=76281980&t=8)** One of the more common tasks that you'll find with dirty data is needing to preprocess it before you can do anything useful with it. Let's take this first scenario here where you have some data and you want to do some exploratory data analysis on it. Before you even get to that step, one of the things you could do is look for missing values. For example, if you ran inside of [[Pandas (Software)|pandas]], look for null values like df.na. If you did find them, it would be up to you as the data scientist to decide, do you want to drop the row, for example? Maybe you have so much data, it's better to just drop those rows or you have a small amount of data and you may want to impute the value. For example, maybe take the median value for the data set and put that value inside. In a second scenario for [[Natural Language Processing (NLP)|Natural Language Processing]], really common to do preprocessing like tokenization, removing stopwords. In this particular scenario, one of the scenarios that a data scientist would encounter is preprocessing before you do sentiment analysis. So for example, removing things like the, and, or because they'll remove the effectiveness of something you're doing for NLP.

#### [Scaling data](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/scaling-data?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/scaling-data?u=76281980&t=8)** Scaling data is one of the more interesting problems with machine learning. Machine learning data in general will require scaling, not all, but majority of machine learning problems require you to scale the data. And there's a very simple intuition behind this. Here's a good example. Let's say you have a data set and it contains a bunch of different columns inside and someone is comparing a value they found in a column, they said, "I ran five." Well, what do you mean? Do you mean you ran 5 miles, 5 kilometers, 5 feet? Really, the difference is significant depending on what metric you're using. That's really the general intuition is one column could be in miles, another one could be in feet. And by doing a comparison, it really is not a great way to do a machine learning problem. Let's say, for example, your clustering, the clustering would not come out correctly. So how you can fix this is through scaling. And one of the things you can do is you could scale the values between 0 and 1, and now you're doing relative comparisons versus this absolute comparison with the wrong metric. So majority of machine learning [[Algorithms]] do require you to scale your data, and the intuition is is fairly straightforward. And once you do this, you're going to be able to put this into, let's say, a K Means Clustering solution.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Algorithms]] (1)
> **Analogies:** for example (1)

### 13. Creating Input Features

#### [Feature engineering concepts](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/feature-engineering-concepts?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/feature-engineering-concepts?u=76281980&t=8)** Here we have a traditional [[Feature Engineering]] problem. In this case, I have the NBA box score and it has a column with total wins for the season. One of the things we can do is repurpose this old feature and convert it into a new feature. In this case, we could look at the Total Wins and determine if it's over a threshold. So depending on how many games there are in a season, you could say, for example, if you have 50 wins, you would have a winning season and that would be a new value called 1. If you had 35 wins, depending on how many games are in the season, again, this could be a zero that would go into this new column called Winning and Losing Record. Once you have this new feature, now what you can do is use it for a prediction. So we could use that as the label, right? that we're trying to predict. And we would say, for example, can we use -- how many points a team would average and use that to predict whether they would have a winning record or losing record in the season? Let's say that you wanted to create this prediction model based on this attribute. This would be one way to go about it, and you could also create new features as well. But the core idea here is that by reusing old features or combining old features together, you can create a new feature and then use that in a machine learning prediction system.
