#### [Developing ML models: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-four-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-four-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Developing ML models. Let's talk through the key terminology for this lesson. To start with, we have transfer learning. The idea with transfer learning is it's a technique where a pretrained model is used and a fine-tuning method for a different but related task leverages that knowledge from the initial training, so the idea here is that you can take something that was built by experts, transform it a little bit, and enhance the quality of that model. In terms of a framework, this is a software or library or platform that provides a structure and reasonable code for developing machine learning models. In terms of semi-supervised machine learning, this is a approach that combines a small amount of labeled data with a large amount of unlabeled data to improve the model performance, and the idea here with semi-supervised machine learning is that many times you don't have all of the label data, but if you're able to use semi-supervised machine learning, that doesn't matter because the existing label data can serve to make a catalyst to label the [[Representational State Transfer (REST)|rest]] of the data. Also, data augmentation is a process of creating new training samples by applying various transformations to the existing data. This would increase the size and the diversity of the data set. In terms of underfitting, this is another very important term to know about. It means that a machine learning model
>
> **[1:34](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-four-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=94)** is failing to capture the underlying patterns in the training data, so it's not really giving you an accurate prediction, and this can result in low accuracy, both on the training data and also on test data sets or even in the real world. Overfitting is when a machine learning model learns too much from the training data, including the noise and random fluctuations, and this will lead to poor generalization on unseen data, so the idea here with overfitting is that you're learning too much, or you're overgeneralizing, and you're making predictions that don't fit in the real world. They only fit with the training set that you have. Hyperparameter tuning is a very important one as well, and this is the idea of using different parameters to tweak and enhance the prediction quality, and we also have [[Vertex]] AI. Vertex AI is a [[MLOps]]-based platform that's developed inside of the [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud platform]], and you can build everything you need to deploy a model by using Vertex AI. Okay, this is the summary of what we covered in terms of key terms in developing models. To summarize here, we talked about frameworks, which are software libraries or platforms that provide structure and reusable code for developing ML models. We also talked about transfer learning, which is a technique that leverages pretrained models for fine-tuning on a different but related task. We also talked about data augmentation,
>
> **[3:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-four-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=188)** creating new training samples by applying transformations to existing data. We dove into semi-supervised machine learning, really, a way to improve model performance. We also talked about overfitting and underfitting. We also talked about hyperparameter tuning, which is the process of optimizing configuration settings of an ML model to improve performance. We also talked about the managed platform, Vertex AI, which you can use to build, deploy, and maintain ML models at scale.

#### [Overfitting vs. underfitting](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/overfitting-vs-underfitting?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/overfitting-vs-underfitting?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at the concept of overfitting versus underfitting. In one way, you can think about a underfitting problem as the line is not directly following the points of a scatter plot. So if this is a scatter plot here, and we have a bunch of data, notice there's a slightly super linear pattern here where there's some data that's appearing up here. And if you're putting your regression line through this part of the dataset, you're missing out on predicting, potentially, some of these other values as they rise up. Likewise, if you wanted to create a more balanced model here, notice that you would have a slight curve to the data, and you can see that the line is appearing directly through the middle of the dataset. So you can call this a balanced prediction. There's a overfitting problem, though, as well, that can occur, and that's when you try to too closely match the data. And so the prediction could be unrealistic because it's not generalizable to other datasets. So it may work for the particular test dataset that you're working on, but if you're kind of following too closely and creating this unusual pattern here, when you get new datasets, that unusual pattern could cause a overfitting problem. So that's really the intuition here, is are you not predicting values well enough because you're trying to be too safe? Is there a balanced approach where you're taking a little bit of risk and making the curve here,
>
> **[1:43](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/overfitting-vs-underfitting?u=76281980&t=103)** or you're getting too risky of a prediction where you're trying to fit the data too closely, and as a result, when it gets new datasets, it won't be able to generalize and create a prediction that makes sense on a wider variety of data.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Definitions:** is a  (1)
> **Speakers:** - [instructor] (1)

#### [Selecting the right metrics](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-the-right-metrics?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-the-right-metrics?u=76281980&t=8)** - [Instructor] One of the classic problems with machine learning is you have to figure out what good is. And how do you do this? Well, one of the ways you can do this is by selecting a metric to figure out for your problem what it is that you're trying to solve. Now, let's take a very simple scenario here where you're trying to predict between cats and dogs. So if you have, let's say, 10 items here and there are some cats and there's some dogs in there, you want to be able to determine how many times you were able to correctly identify a dog, and this would be a true positive, and how many times you were correctly able to identify that it was not a dog, it was a cat, and this would be the false negative. So you can see this is a relevant element, so it's this category. Now, in the alternate scenario here, if you're able to, in this scenario say that these were dogs but there were really cats, this would be a false positive. In the case of a true negative, this would also be a similar scenario. So how do we determine this? Well, in terms of metrics, we have precision, how many retrieved items were relevant? So in this category here, how many of them was I successfully able to retrieve? And then in terms of recall, how many relevant items are actually retrieved? So basically, if you were not able to correctly identify all of the items in your data, then you're going to have a limited recall.
>
> **[1:40](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-the-right-metrics?u=76281980&t=100)** So maybe it isn't as useful to use this particular model because there's a whole category of data that you weren't able to actually successfully do a recall on. So this really manifests itself in something called the area under the curve. If we go over to Wikipedia here, take a look at a, you know, essentially a perfect classifier system, one of the things that we can see from a perfect classifier is that on a true positive rate and a false positive rate, we're able to get this perfect curve here where we are able to always identify a true positive and always identify a false positive, but in the real world, this is often not the case. If we have a random classifier here, you can see this would be not great performance, right? This is just randomly selecting between one category or another. As you increase here, the ability to select between true positive and false positive rate, you can see we get better, or in this, if we go in this direction, we get worse. And so that's really one of the key intuitions with classification is the area under the curve or ROC curve, and we want to select what is the correct balance that will give us the best ability to say these are cats, these are dogs, and eliminate false positives, and also, select for true positives. Now, if we go to SageMaker,
>
> **[3:14](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-the-right-metrics?u=76281980&t=194)** another area that we can use metrics for is by looking at numerical predictions. So in the case of this particular model inside of SageMaker Canvas, you can see that on the left here we have actual values of medium house, and then we have predicted value. So we have this metric here that is able to show us in terms of root means squared error, that this model often predicts that the value is between this particular range, which is within 45,000 of the actual value. So, that really depends on what kind of housing market you're in, whether that would be a good enough model for you. And if we go to advanced metrics, we can dig into a little bit more of the different problems and get a visual relationship. If we look at R-squared, which is called the goodness of fit, we can see that in this particular scenario, We can see what percentage of the difference in median house price can be explained by the input column. So essentially, of the picture that we have, how much of it is explained by the features that we selected. In this case, we see that it's close to 80%. So we can explain roughly 80% of what was going on by the features that we've selected. So it means that it's possible to improve it a little bit more if we got additional features inside of the data. And let's go back here
>
> **[4:45](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/selecting-the-right-metrics?u=76281980&t=285)** and look at the build selection here. You can see the different columns here, right? So we can see that, in fact, we have rooms, bedrooms, population, ocean proximity, median income, longitude, latitude, right? So these columns here explain 80%. And if we go to analyze here, one of the things that we can do as well is we can even see the impact of each of these features on the model. So there are lots of ways to dig into the metrics and not just look at the core metric but the supporting metrics to determine whether you have been able to do the cost benefit analysis of the prediction successfully. There is no perfect metric. You often need to look at multiple metrics to successfully predict via machine learning.

#### [Fine-tuning raw ingredients with Hugging Face](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/fine-tuning-raw-ingredients-hugging-face?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/fine-tuning-raw-ingredients-hugging-face?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at the key components to fine-tuning a pre-trained model. One of the things to be aware of is you need an environment to fine tune. In fact, a GPU is the ideal scenario to fine-tune it. [[GitHub]] [[Codespaces]] is a great environment. It has GPU access for education and it allows you to quickly go through and fine tune things. The three things that you care about, typically, are your own accuracy metrics. So, are you trying to move towards a classification metric for example, like ROUGE or precision and recall, et cetera. Do you need to do a local checkpoint as well? So, are you going to periodically make these modifications as you're getting better and better accuracy to the model? And then, do you have some code that's going to be associated with your fine-tuning process? Really, these are the components, right? GPU, accuracy, local checkpoint, and code. Now, in terms of pulling things down from [[Hugging Face]], a few things to be aware of is typically, it's going to be the tokenizer, the model, the dataset. These are the raw ingredients. Just like when you're cooking something, you'd need flour and butter and sugar. It's a similar concept here. Tokenizer, model, dataset. You would then go through here, put it through your system that fine-tunes it, makes it a little bit better, and then you push that back to Hugging Face. Really, this is the concept of fine-tuning and it's a fairly straightforward process if you have all of these raw ingredients set up.

#### [Advantages of transfer learning](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/advantages-transfer-learning?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/advantages-transfer-learning?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Let's take a look at some of the advantages of transfer learning and why it's so different from traditional [[Supervised Learning]]. In supervised machine learning, a lot of the problems come up with access to data. Do you have the ability to process the data? Do you have the data? And also the compute, do you have the ability to access the compute, and can you use that compute to process things? If we look at a particular problem here with a news data set, you would go through and train it on, let's say, a historical model, and then serve out a prediction that could summarize the data. Likewise, you would need to pick a separate data set, summarize it, and then create a prediction. So it involves a lot of computational resources, storage resources, in order to train things with traditional supervised machine learning. In the NLP space, one of the advantages of transfer learning is that you can take something that was trained from a different domain, and in this case, this was a news dataset. And we could keep the body here, so we could initialize a new model that is going to be fine tuned with a literature dataset, and then we would tweak the head here by removing it and then actually [[Fine Tuning]] against some new data that had the literature information inside of it. This would then give us a more efficient way to do a prediction. So the difference here is that fine tuning allows you to take something that was developed by experts and developed with a lot of computational
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/advantages-transfer-learning?u=76281980&t=99)** and storage resources and then fine tune it on your own resources. [[Hugging Face]] is one of the premier places where the fine tuning process takes place, and it allows you to do high quality model-based training efficiently on another domain. So really, in the future, we'll see more and more advantages of transfer learning in the marketplace, and in fact, you can actually do transfer learning yourself and push this onto Hugging Face as a fine tune model.

> [!info]- Semantic Content

#### [Operationalize microservices](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/operationalize-microservices?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/operationalize-microservices?u=76281980&t=5)** - [Instructor] Here we have a diagram of operationalizing a microservice. Notice that in [[Git]], some of the advantages of a microservice come alive. I really like to think about a microservice in terms of the concept of logic that you go live with. Some of the characteristics are that it is small and it's reusable code, so you can maybe turn this into a command line tool. You can turn it into a library. You can, of course, make a microservice out of it. You can also convert it into a dock or container. All of these are characteristics of a microservice. It's really like a function that you've operationalized and turned into something new. Now, if you go to the [[Continuous Delivery (CD)|continuous delivery]] system or the build server, it'll go through the steps of linting, testing, compiling, maybe pushing this into a container repository, and then finally deploying through [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|infrastructure as code]]. What this does by using infrastructure's code coupled with, let's say, a container, is that you can create any number of environments. You can create a staging environment. You can create a production environment. You can create a load test environment. You can create a development environment. And you can do automated things depending on which environment it gets deployed to. So in the case of the staging environment, and this is something I've done in my career, you'd have a microservice that has health metrics like CPU, memory, latency. These are all monitored in some kind of monitoring system like Prometheus or potentially Cloud Wash from AWS. And then you do a performance verification, and what this is really doing is verifying
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/operationalize-microservices?u=76281980&t=99)** that what you think will happen will actually happen. Once this is actually approved, you can easily just merge this into a new branch and push it directly into production. So really, a microservice, the simplest possible explanation is it's logic that you push live.

#### [Automating and orchestrating ML pipelines: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-five-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-five-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** - [Instructor] Automating and orchestrating ML pipelines. Let's talk through the key terminology. First step here, we can talk about orchestration frameworks. These are important because they're a platform that automate and manage the execution of complex multi-step workflows. Like ML pipelines. Training pipelines are a series of automated processes that handle different stages of ML model development including [[Data Preparation]], training, and evaluation. There are hybrid and multi-cloud strategies that are important to consider. Let's first start with [[Hybrid Cloud]]. This is a computing environment that combines your on-prem data center also with a private cloud. This private cloud and public cloud enables the data and applications to be shared across whatever strategy makes sense. A multi-cloud is a little bit different in that is a cloud only strategy where you would use multiple cloud offerings from different providers maybe using [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|infrastructure as code]] and services like [[Kubernetes]] for orchestration to avoid vendor lock and increase your flexibility, but at a cost of increased complexity of managing that system. Model versioning is a practice of tracking and managing multiple iterations of a machine learning model through the lifecycle. For example, you're training it improving new things with the model, and you keep different versions. We see this with LLMs quite a bit. For example, we have GPT three, GPT four.
>
> **[1:33](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-five-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=93)** This is a great example of model versioning. Serving pipelines are a little bit different because they're responsible for deploying a trained machine learning model to a production environment and this makes them available for online or batch predictions. An important consideration also is what is the performance of the serving pipeline, and can you actually do binary based deployment, which is an emerging standard? And also, can you use a cross-platform model serving technology like Onyx? Dataset lineage and [[Metadata]] really refer to information about what's happening in your ML system. First up, we have Lineage, which refers to the history and relationship between different versions of a data set including how it's been transformed and used in various machine learning models. Metadata is descriptive information about data like the format, the source, the lineage, and it can be used to track and manage data sets and ML models. In terms of summary, let's dive into the details here. The key terms to be aware of for automating and orchestrating ML pipelines include thinking about the components, the parameters, the triggers and the compute needs. Also choosing the correct orchestration framework for whatever [[Cloud Strategy]] you have. Also, serving pipelines so when do you want to serve out the data? Do you want to do it via online, batch or caching? And what are the different [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] serving options? Also tracking and auditing metadata. Can you organize and track experiments of pipeline runs?
>
> **[3:09](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-five-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=189)** And then in terms of monitoring, optimizing and maintaining ML solutions, do you understand how to troubleshoot for performance and business quality of predictions? And can you tune the performance for training and serving in production?

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Metadata]] (3), [[Data Preparation]] (1), [[Hybrid Cloud]] (1), [[Infrastructure as code (IaC)|Infrastructure as code]] (1), [[Kubernetes]] (1)
> **Definitions:** is a  (5), is an  (1), refers to (1)
> **Env Vars:** gpt (2)
> **Analogies:** for example (2)
> **Warnings:** be aware (1), troubleshoot (1)

#### [Understanding TPUs](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/understanding-tpus?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/understanding-tpus?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] Let's look at the official documentation for a cloud-based TPU. TPUs are [[Hardware]] accelerators designed by [[Google]] for machine learning workloads. And we can also look at the system's architecture here to get a little bit more information. So if we go into that link, and we see, in terms of what you can do with it, that in fact, you can use [[TensorFlow]], [[PyTorch]], and JAX. So there's three different frameworks that are supported. TensorFlow is really the classic one that's been out for a bit, and JAX is the new version that uses things on top of NumPy, and PyTorch is another framework as well. Now, the idea here to take a look at is that the latest version is TPU version 4. And TPU version 4 contains two TensorCores. And what this means is that in terms of a peak performance here, we've got 275 teraflops, and there are 4,096 chips. So the idea here is that there's a huge amount of density here, where you're able to train matrix multiplication-type problems at a optimized level. You can see that it's built on other versions. So there's TPU version 3 here. There's TPU version 2. And if you look at what it looks like, that you're essentially building on top of lots of cores so that you can do matrix multiplication at scale. And in terms of the cloud architecture here,
>
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/understanding-tpus?u=76281980&t=102)** let's take a look at how this would work. So you could, in fact, use a TPU board, and this is connected to a CPU host. You are going to have to do some operations yourself in terms of a CPU-based host machine, but with a TPU pod, there is a host for each TPU board. So it really depends on what it is that you're trying to do. If we go into a node here, the TPU node consists of a user VM that communicates with the host over gRPC. And when you're using this architecture, you can't directly communicate with the host. So the idea here is you're just dispatching what you're doing to really dedicated architecture for doing matrix multiplication. With a VM, this enables you to SSH into the VM physically that's connected to the TPU, and you have root access as well. So it really depends on what it is you're trying to do. This has got a slightly more bit of overhead, but you can debug it potentially a little bit easier. Now, if we go down here and we take a look at the supported frameworks here, what's very fascinating about this, again, is that there's a shared runtime via this library called libtpu. This libtpu here allows you to SSH in, for example, with a TPU host. And you could do TensorFlow, you could do JAX, you could do PyTorch. Essentially, it goes down into this library, and this library is able to actually translate it and bring it down in terms of running on this TPU board. So in a nutshell here,
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/understanding-tpus?u=76281980&t=197)** this is the key component of the architecture. Now, if we take a look at why we're doing this, I think this is really critical in terms of understanding how a CPU works. So a CPU is a general purpose processor, and von Neumann actually, in the early days of computing, helped build this architecture. And it means that you can do regular things with a computer, like [[Microsoft Word|word]] processing and manipulating files. Really, the use case is a flexibility, right? This is the goal of a CPU. A GPU is interesting because it actually is accidentally successful in terms of [[Deep Learning]] because of the fact that it has this density, right? So instead of there being one or maybe eight chips for a CPU, you have, let's say, 2,500 to 5,000 different cores here that you can actually do different processing calculations on. And what's nice about this is you can execute thousands of multiplications and additions simultaneously. So that's really the idea here with a GPU architecture. But what if you actually needed to go to another level and actually not use a general purpose processor here? Well, that's where the TPU comes in. And the idea here is that it's optimized for doing matrix operations. And the TPU itself is able to have this really high-density calculations by using the TPU.
>
> **[4:50](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/understanding-tpus?u=76281980&t=290)** So really, that's the TPU in a nutshell, is that it's a specialized chip specifically designed for doing deep learning at scale. And that's why when you use it with, let's say, the [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud Platform]] and TensorFlow or JAX, you're going to get a better efficiency than just using a GPU alone.

#### [TPUs as a technology transition](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/tpus-as-technology-transition?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:06](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/tpus-as-technology-transition?u=76281980&t=6)** - [Instructor] The [[TensorFlow]] Processing Unit or a TPU, is a classic technology transition. Let's take a look at how it compares with other technology transitions. In phase one, we have an internal combustion engine. It's been around for quite some time, and the internal combustion engine got better and better. It got the ability to get better gas mileage, but eventually there's a point where a new transition occurred where some of the limitations of internal combustion engine, for example that it runs on oil, that it also creates pollution. Those types of issues have led to a transition in technology. And if we take a look at the internal combustion engine that is mixed with a battery, like a hybrid car, it has this transitionary capability of both using the new stuff, right? The newer battery as it's getting developed, but also the legacy internal combustion engine. Finally, with some of the modern advances with electric cars, they're pure battery, and in many scenarios you can see that the electric cars is much faster, has longer range than an internal combustion engine. So it's part of the typical technology transition. If we look at a CPU, it's similar in that a CPU is designed for doing multiple things at the same time in terms of, you know, working with [[Microsoft Word|word]] processing software, or you know allowing someone to load a file, or to calculate something.
>
> **[1:38](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/tpus-as-technology-transition?u=76281980&t=98)** But it isn't designed for parallel processing at scale. It's a multipurpose CPU and it's designed for really kind of desktop type operations. And as we've transitioned into the [[Deep Learning]] world and AI world, you can see that the deep learning stack currently is, there's CPUs, and there's GPUs, and they're kind of mixed together. And a GPU is very good at doing parallel processing but it wasn't designed for doing parallel processing. It's accidentally good at doing deep learning. But the future is, and TPU is one of the examples but there's also lots of other companies that are doing custom silicone, is that there's a chip that's specifically optimized for the problem at hand. In the case of a TPU, it's optimized for matrix multiplication and it works very well with JAX, TensorFlow, [[PyTorch]], and so it's new technology without the legacy baggage of the old system. Likewise, we also can see this with languages. Originally C was developed as a systems programming language, and it is wildly successful. It's been around for decades, and decades, but the speed of development is a little bit slow. There's all kinds of safety issues and the skill level required is very high. And so people have used things like [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] on top of C where there's numerical libraries in C, and it's a transitionary technology, right? So you're wrapping up C with Python, making it easier.
>
> **[3:12](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/tpus-as-technology-transition?u=76281980&t=192)** And that's currently what we're doing with ML Stack. But what we're seeing is that in the final destination here what may happen is that modern compiled languages, let's say for example, [[Rust (Programming Language)|Rust]], or Go, that have built in the last 10 years, that the whole stack could be written in Rust. And we see this, for example, with PyTorch, there's examples of using deep learning libraries or even rewriting the entire thing from scratch with one language. So the transition of technology, a lot of times you'll see this phase where there's a mixture of a legacy technology, and potentially a hybrid solution, or a newer solution, and eventually it turns into a new thing. So the TPUs are a part of this technology transition.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Deep Learning]] (4), [[TensorFlow]] (2), [[PyTorch]] (2), [[Python (Programming Language)|Python]] (2), [[Rust (Programming Language)|Rust]] (2)

#### [Monitoring, optimizing, and maintaining ML solutions: Key terminology](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-six-key-terminology?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:00](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-six-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=0)** Monitoring, optimizing, and maintaining ML solutions. Let's dive into the key terminology for this lesson. First up, we have monitoring and logging strategies. The idea here with monitoring is that it involves tracking the performance and quality of machine learning models and production. It ensures that they meet business requirements and maintain accuracy over time. You can think of it as [[Data Science]], right? Data science for software engineering systems. Logging strategies define how an ML system records and stores, information about model predictions, errors, and other relevant events for analysis and debugging purposes. Also, we have continuous evaluation metrics and [[Google Cloud Platform (GCP)|Google Cloud]] permission models. If we look at continuous evaluation metrics, these are measures used to assess the ongoing performance of ML models, including the detection of a drift or a bias in model predictions. The also inclusive cloud permissions model will govern access to how things are controlled and authorized for various Google Cloud services, the resources and operations in an ML system. There is retraining policy and model drift, two other important components to discuss. A retraining policy determines when and how often an ML model should be trained using new or updated data to maintain its performance and accuracy.
>
> **[1:32](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-six-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=92)** Model drift occurs when the underlying data distribution changes over time, causing a decline in the performance and accuracy of a machine learning model. Oftentimes you can detect this by looking at the percentage of data that's changed. We also have dataset lineage and [[Metadata]] to consider. These are also very critical when you're thinking about operationalizing and maintaining systems at scale. And dataset lineage and metadata are very important in terms of looking at the descriptive [[Statistics]] of a monitoring system and making sure they apply when that model is deployed into production. All right. Let's talk about what we've discussed in this terminology section here. The key terms in automating and orchestrating ML pipelines include monitoring, which is tracking the performance and quality of ML models and production. Logging strategies approaching are the ways to record and store information about model predictions and errors. Continuous evaluation metrics, this is measuring how to assess the ongoing performance of models. Retraining policy, which are guidelines determining how and often an ML model should be retrained. We also have model drift, which is the performance of models and how they decline over time. We have model bias, which is systemic errors in ML model predictions and also [[TensorFlow]] errors. And these are common issues that arise during training and serving of solutions built during the TensorFlow Framework process.
>
> **[3:10](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/course-six-key-terminology?u=76281980&t=190)** All right. That's it for this particular lesson. Let's go ahead and get started.

### 25. ML Solutions

#### [Data drift explained by the naughty child problem](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-drift-explained-by-naughty-child-problem?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/data-drift-explained-by-naughty-child-problem?u=76281980&t=5)** Here's a great example of one of the problems with data drift in the real world. This is from Nassim Taleb, who is the author of The Black Swan and also other books on [[Probability]]. One of the examples he gives in his books is this concept of when you grab more balls out of a container, each new ball gives you more information about the frequency of the red ball versus the black ball, right? And so in this case, we see things are static, and so the more information you get, the better it is. But in the real world, because data is dynamically changing, it isn't necessarily the case that you're always going to get an accurate representation of what the state of the world is. And one example he brings up is imagine the same scenario where the bottom has an access panel and there's a child in there that's constantly filling the access panel with new balls, right? It could potentially be disrupting your knowledge, right? Even though you're grabbing more and more balls out of the container, it actually doesn't give you any more information because the distribution of the black balls versus the red balls is constantly changing, right? And so this is really the core idea with data drift, is that if the data is constantly changing, you may not actually have the information you think you have. And this is why retraining a model constantly in many scenarios is the best situation to address this concept of the ever changing underlying data structure.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Probability]] (1)
> **Analogies:** imagine (1)

#### [Load testing with Locust](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/load-testing-with-locust?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:08](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/load-testing-with-locust?u=76281980&t=8)** Locust is an open source [[Load Testing]] tool that can actually do millions of simultaneous users. #### [Five whys](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/five-whys?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:05](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/five-whys?u=76281980&t=5)** Plan, Do, Check, and Act. It's a implementation of the scientific method, but applied to a manufacturing or software engineering context. First, in the planning phase, you need to identify the problem. So we need to build an automation tool that looks at inventory. Next, you go ahead and try that solution out. Once you've done the initial prototype, you want to check to make sure it actually accomplishes the business goal, right? So if you had some automation around inventory, it should have an accurate count of the inventory. So you'd go through and analyze the results. If, in fact it does work, then you would act by putting this into a production component of your facility. If it doesn't work, you would need to go through, go back again to the planning phase, identify what the problems are, and then go back and repeat this process. So really the Plan, Do, Check, Act is an iterative process that constantly improves things. And this is a key component of the Kaizen methodology. This is an example from my career in a real scenario that was very serious. We had servers that were randomly crashing. And so the first question is, why do they crash? The answer is not sure. There are no log files, so we can't investigate why there's a crash. The next question to ask is, why are there no log files?
>
> **[1:39](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/five-whys?u=76281980&t=99)** Once that question had been posed, we were able to discover that the systems were using Ephemeral storage, which is a storage that will go away if it reboots and there was no SWAP partition. This is the partition that's used when memory is basically consumed on a machine. And so then the next question we had was, why are there systems that are using Ephemeral storage without SWAP? Next, the question was resolved with the answer of [[DevOps]] specialist used to work at a top tech company and they said they don't use SWAP there. And the second answer was that Ephemeral storage is cheaper. So then the natural follow up is what would happen if we enable SWAP and then use EBS storage. EBS storage is block storage that is persistent across reboots. The final root cause analysis was that we were able to show that there was a Kernel panic and an Out Of Memory Error in the crash logs. This is the last step here to debug the problem. And then we asked the question, what is causing that error? And now we can see that the root causes that the [[Java]] Application [[Server Configuration]] file allocates more memory than the machine has available. So it was causing an issue where the application software itself consumed all the memory and because there's no
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/five-whys?u=76281980&t=197)** SWAP to write to, then the Kernel itself would have to actually come into play. And this is where Part B says, "The Java kernel consumed." Since Java consumes all the memory, the [[Linux]] kernel kills the Java process. Finally, a supervisor, which is managing processes and restarting them automatically, then created a race condition that crashed the machines. So by asking these five questions, it was a very, very difficult problem to solve. But it did get us to the root cause. And not every problem can be solved by Five Whys, but many problems can be solved by Five Whys. And this is really the principle of the heart of Kaizen, which is go to the root cause and continuously find out what that cause is and fix it.

> [!info]- Semantic Content
>
> **Frameworks & Libraries:** [[Java]] (4), [[DevOps]] (1), [[Server Configuration]] (1), [[Linux]] (1)
> **Env Vars:** swap (5), ebs (2)
> **Definitions:** is an  (2), is a  (2)
> **CLI Commands:** make (1), find (1)
> **UI Navigation:** go to (1)

#### [Using Google Courses](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/using-google-courses?u=76281980)

>

#### [Building a CUDA GPU stress test](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980)

> [!transcript]- Transcript
>
> **[0:03](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980&t=3)** One of the more powerful ways to use [[Rust (Programming Language)|Rust]] is to build a systems tool that talks to a GPU. >
> **[1:42](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980&t=102)** to do a full stress test. All right. Let's go ahead and get started. Three, two, one. Okay. Let's go ahead and get -- open this environment here. I'm going to go ahead and launch it. And we can actually very easily get started with building out this stress test and some of the things to take care of right out of the way is to make sure that we've got the correct cargo structure set up. That's really one of the key takeaways here. And it's probably easy to go through and first run this to see how it works and then we can see what are the changes we want to make in the future. So here I have a translate app that's one, and I also have a stress app. I'm going to go ahead and use the stress one. And this one is pretty interesting because if we look at the cargo file, we're using three dependencies. First up, we have the command line tool. Second, we have the PyTorch bindings for Rust, and then we have rayon, which allows us to do multithreaded code. That's all I have to do to build this stress test tool. Next up, I again use this pattern. My library code lives in lib.rs and my commandline tool lives in main. Let's go ahead and take a look at the library code first. Here, inside of the library code, we have functions that are built, and each function is quite simple. So I have the rayon imports, I have the [[TensorFlow]] imports, but if we look at this, you can see that I build a load test and all it's doing is sending a vector into the device.
>
> **[3:17](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980&t=197)** In this case, it's a CPU and it's going to print out what's happening. In this case, I build a gou_load_test function, so we just change the device. So we say, look, I want to actually iterate into this CUDA device. And then finally, all I need to do to change it to have a multithreaded version of this is just use this into_par_iter for each pub and then throw that into the CUDA device as well. So what's really cool about this is we have three completely different ways to actually utilize testing. Now, all you need to do is go over to my main and again, it's the exact same pattern. This is essentially boilerplate code. Here's the stuff that matters. I build out a CPU subcommand, I build out a GPU subcommand, and then I build out a TGPU subcommand for multithreaded and look, I just map each one of those to the library code. So this pattern is so powerful because you build just a few lines of code in a library and then you map that those functions into here. The only other thing you have to do to be aware of is make sure that these are each pub. Once you've done that, you're good to go. Now, all I need to do to run this is go ahead and run cargo. So let's go ahead and build that. We'll say cargo and then say --help and we can say cargo run help, and this will go through and compile it again. It'll give us a help menu which will correspond directly to
>
> **[4:55](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980&t=295)** each of these commands, the CPU, the GPU, and the TGPU. And that's exactly what we see. Now, one of the things that's really helpful is to do two kinds of monitoring. First up, let's go ahead and do each top and let's go ahead and take a look at the CPU here. So if we go through here and we look at the CPU and I run cargo run and I say CPU, what's going to happen is it's going to throw a bunch of data into the CPUs and look, you can see it's saturated, right? Now, if I go over to the GPU monitoring, you'll see nothing's happening, right? So this is a great way to understand what's going on with your code, right? We know that we're using the CPU device, so no traffic is going to the GPU. So next, what I'm going to do is now we're going to change gears here. You can see it stopped and I'm going to go through and I'm going to say, "Okay. Let's go to the GPU." Now, what happens when you go to the GPU? We do a GPU stress test and lombo hold, it's going to send a bunch of data into the GPU. We'll be able to see it by toggling right here and you'll see this thing getting saturated. Now, it only really gets up to, let's say, a quarter of the load. And if we look at the threads here as well, they're still being utilized. So basically the cores are still being utilized, but some traffic as well as being sent to the GPU. Now, can we do better than this?
>
> **[6:27](https://www.linkedin.com/learning/google-cloud-professional-machine-learning-engineer-cert-prep/building-a-cuda-gpu-stress-test?u=76281980&t=387)** Can we get this load higher? If we really want to stress test things? Well, we can and that's where the threaded GPU comes into play. And that was the code I wrote earlier that uses rayon. And now let's take a look at this. There we go. So we've been able to actually over more than double the load that's getting sent to the GPU, right? It was really around like 20, 25%. So let's say it's been two to three times more load. And now if we look at the CPU, check this out. Notice how it's actually reduced the load of the CPU. So you can see here, once you start putting enough traffic into a GPU, it's actually a great way to offload some of the resources on your system. So what's really great about doing stress testing for a tool like this is to really get a feel for what's happening in your environment. Unless you do a benchmark, you really don't know what's happening, especially in a distributed computing problem like doing PyTorch training on a GPU. And you can see here, Rust is the perfect tool to do training, to do inference, to build portable binary tools. And really Rust is the systems tool that is an ideal tool for [[MLOps]].
